Transfer to Metaphorical Meaning When Literal Is Impossible
Explains the rule for transferring to metaphorical meaning when literal is impossible, using examples from oaths and freeing slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that when literal meaning (haqiqah) is impossible, the utterance transfers to its metaphorical meaning (majaz) based on contextual understanding, and distinguish between metaphor (isti'ara) and eloquent simile (tashbih baligh) in legal interpretation.

Core Idea
When literal meaning is impossible, the utterance transfers to metaphorical meaning if contextually understood — intention is not required.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi usul principle: how to interpret an utterance when its literal meaning is impossible. The classical jurists distinguished between cases where the literal is inherently impossible (e.g., touching the sky) and cases where it is only contingently impossible (e.g., drinking from an empty jug). The discussion leads to the rule that transfer to metaphorical meaning depends only on the possibility of understanding the literal, not on the speaker's intention. This principle has major implications for oaths, acknowledgements, and the interpretation of metaphors in legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath scenarios
Explain the difference between swearing to touch the sky (inherently impossible) and swearing to drink from an empty jug (currently impossible but drinking is generally possible). Note the ruling: no expiation vs expiation.
The first oath is not contracted; the second is contracted because the literal act is generally possible.
2
Extract the general rule
The transfer from literal to metaphorical depends on understanding (fahm) of the literal meaning, not on intention (irada). One cannot intend both literal and metaphorical simultaneously.
The rule is: 'Al-intiqal min al-haqiqah ila al-majaz mawquf 'ala fahm al-awwal, la 'ala iradatih.'
3
Apply to the 'son' example
When a master says 'this is my son' to a slave, literal sonship is impossible, so it transfers to the necessary concomitant: emancipation. No intention required; it is determined (muta'ayyin).
The slave is freed judicially (qada'an) without needing the master's intention.
4
Distinguish metaphor and simile
In predicate constructions like 'Zayd is a lion', it is an eloquent simile (tashbih baligh), not a true metaphor, because the claim of identity is impossible; an implicit 'like' is assumed. Similarly, 'this is my son' if interpreted as a simile does not produce emancipation.
True metaphor (isti'ara) requires the object of comparison to be omitted.
5
Understand the nature of metaphor (isti'ara)
Metaphor occurs first in meaning then in words, so it does not apply to proper nouns. In true metaphor, the thing compared (musabbah) is omitted, e.g., 'I saw a lion shooting an arrow.'
The context (qarinah) indicates the metaphorical meaning.

انتقال الى اللازم جو کہ بدرجہ مجاز ہے یہ صرف معنی حقیقی کی نہم پر موقوف ہے یعنی وہ الفاظ ایسے ہوں کہ معنی حقیقی اس سے مفہوم ہو سکے اس فہم معنی میں کوئی خلجان نہ ہو اور معنی حقیقی کے ارادہ ہو سکنے پر موقوف نہیں ہے کیونکہ معنی حقیقی اور مجازی کے مابین جمع فی الارادہ نہیں ہوا کرتی ۔ فاذا لم يتوقف یه انتقال جب اراده اول پر موقوف ہی نہیں تو امکان اول واجب نہیں ہے یہ اس وقت ضروری ہوتا جب اراده اول ضروری ہوتا وحيث توقف على فهم الاول جب انتقال الى المجاز صرف فهم اول عني معنی حقیقی کے صرف فہم پر موقوف ہے تو فہم اول صرف اس پر موقوف ہے کہ لفظ لال من حيث العربیہ صحیح ہو تو انتقال الى المجازہ کے لئے کلام کا من حیث العربیہ صحیح ہونا کافی ہے اور من حیث المعنی کی صحتہ اور امکان کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ ناذا نعم الدول جب الفاظ سے معنی حقیقی مفہوم ہو لیکن اس کا ارادہ نہ ہو سکے خواہ اس لئے کہ وہ غلام معروف النسب ہے یا مولی سے عمر میں زیادہ ہے ۔ علم ان المراد تو معلوم ہو جائے گا کہ مراد اس کا لازم ہے اور نبدا اپنی کا لازم عشق ہے تو یہ عقیق ہی مراد ہوگا۔ من حین ملکہ اسی وقت سے جب کا وہ اس کا مالک ہوا اور یہی عشق بنوة کا لازم ہے ۔ فیجعل اقراراً پس یہ لفظ ہذا اپنی اقرار بالعتق ہوگا اور یہ غلام بلانیتہ قضاء ازار ہو جائیں گا ۔ لانہ متعین کیونکہ یہی معنی عشق اس وقت میں ہے اور اس کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے معنی کا احتمال نہیں ہے لہذا متکلم کی نیت کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے ۔ ولا یعنق بقول یا اپنی اگر بجائے ہذا اپنی کے اپنے غلام کو یا اپنی بصورة ندا کتنا ہے تو اس صورت میں غلام آزاد نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ ندا صرف منادی کو بصورة اسم استحضار کی خاطر ہوتا ہے اور معنی الفاظ مقصود نہیں ہوتے لہذا تصحیح معنی کی خاطر استعارہ جاری نہیں ہو گا تو تصحیح معنی کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ فان الاستعارة تقع اولانی المعنی یعنی استعاره اولا معنی میں ہوتا ہے پھر الفاظ میں بواسطہ معنی کے ہوتا ہے ۔ جب رجل شجاع کے لئے بطور استعارہ لفظ اسد بولا جائے تو اولاً لفظ اسد کا جو معنی ہے یعنی سیکل مخصوص اولاً اس کا استعارہ رجل شجاع کے لئے کیا جائے گا۔ پھر اس استعارہ کے توسط سے لفظ اسد کا استعارہ برائے رجل شجاع کیا جائے گا ۔ لاجل ان الاستعارہ چونکہ استعارہ اوّلاً معنی میں واقع ہوتا ہے ۔ ثانیاً لفظ میں اس لئے استعارہ اعلام میں جاری نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ علم کے اندر معنی مقصود نہیں ہوتا البتہ وہ جو مخصوص معنی پر عر فا دال ہیں۔ جیسے لفظ عائم جو دال علی السخاوہ ہے ان کا استعارہ ہو سکتا ہے۔ ریحقق بقوله ياحر پھر اس پر اشکال ہوتا تھا کہ جب یا اپنی بصورت ندا ساتھ عشق ثابت نہیں ہوتا تو پھر یا حر کے ساتھ بھی ثابت نہیں ہونا چاہئے۔ تو ان کا جواب دیا کہ یا حر کے ساتھ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ خود عشق اور حریتہ کے معنی کے لئے موضوع ہے اور اسقاط رقیہ کے لئے یہ خود بدرجہ علم ہو چکا ہے۔ لہذا یہاں کوئی استعارہ اور مجاز و غیرہ نہیں ہے۔ خان قبل اگر سوال کیا جائے کہ علم البیان میں مذکور ہے کہ استعادہ مبتدا کی خبر میں جاری نہیں ہوتا ۔ تحوز ید است تو یہ استعارہ نہیں ہے ۔ بلکہ آلہ تشبیہ کے بغیر تشبیہ ہے۔ جسے تشبیہ بلیغ کہتے ہیں کیونکہ ایک تو یہ کہ یہ امر مستحیل کا دعوی ہے اور دوسرا یہ کہ خبریں تصدیق و تکذیب دونوں خبر کی طرف متوجہ ہو سکتے ہیں تو خبر جب ان قبیلہ مستحیلات ہو تو اس کی استقامت کے لئے حرف تشبیہ مقدر ماننا پڑے گا تاکہ خبر استحالہ سے نکل کر استقامہ کی طرف آجاتے لہذا زیڈ اسد کو زید کالاسد قرار دینا پڑے گا۔ تو یہ تشبیہ ہو جائیگی ۔ استعارہ جاری نہیں ہو سکتا۔ لہذا ہذا اپنی چونکہ اس جیسی کلام ہے۔ اور لفظ اپنی خبر مبتداء ہے لہذا یہ استعارہ اور محارض النفق نہیں ہو سکتا۔ بلکہ تشبیہ ہوگی تقدیر یوں ہوگی ہذا مثل اپنی اور تشبیہ میں عشق نہیں ہوسکتا ۔ وانما يكون الاستعارة اور استعارہ وہاں ہو سکتا ہے جہاں مشبہ محذوف ہو اور مشبہ بہ مذکور جیسے رایت اسلایی می تو یہاں مشہو یعنی رجل شجاع محذوف ہے اور مشبہ یہ لفظ اسر مذکور ہے تو بقرینہ پیر کی یہ استعاره عن الشجاع ہو گا کیونکہ یہ می اسد حقیقی کا کام نہیں ہے بلکہ رجل شجاع کا ہے . وان كان هذا مستحيلاً اگر چہ یہ بھی امر مستحیل ہے لیکن یہ استعارہ ہوگا۔ بواسطه القرينة به جار مجرور يكون الاستعارہ کے متعلق ہے . لكنه غیر مقصود یعنی اگر چہ اس کا حقیقی معنی مستحیل ہے لیکن یہ مقصود نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس مقام پر مقصود اصلی رویہ ہے جو کہ مستحیل نہیں ہے۔ فعلی ہذا پس اس
Certainly cannot do it, so he becomes sinful. Expiation becomes obligatory. And if he swears, 'I will not drink the water that is in this jug,' when a person swears an oath, says, 'By God, I will definitely drink the water that is in this jug,' even though there is no water in the jug at all, then here expiation is not obligatory because the content of the oath, which is the fundamental matter, is impossible at the time of the oath. Therefore neither the oath is contracted nor is expiation obligatory. Al-mustashhid: So these two issues — one is touching the sky, the second is drinking water — and the difference between them that has been presented, this is the evidence. The issue of this is for them: in the greater meaning, the original is impossible, so emancipation does not occur; and in the smaller meaning, because it is possible, so he becomes free. The matn, i.e. al-Tanqih, does not mention the issue of the jug, only the issue of touching the sky is presented. Since in our books both are mentioned together commonly and famously, so mentioning one is sufficient because one indicates the other. We say: It is dependent on understanding the first. With the proof, the answer of the Sahibayn is that the transfer from the first to the second is dependent on understanding the first, not on intending it. It is not dependent on the first intention. It is not possible to combine between the literal and metaphorical meaning in intention. And what is meant by literal and metaphorical here is real meaning and figurative meaning. Summary of the answer: That the meaning for which it was coined which is literal, and as a necessary concomitant from it transfer to the necessary which is metaphorical, this is only dependent on the understanding of the literal meaning, i.e., that the words are such that the literal meaning can be understood from them; there should be no confusion in this understanding, and it is not dependent on the possibility of intending the literal meaning, because literal and metaphorical meanings are not combined in intention. So when the transfer is not dependent on the first intention, then the possibility of the first is not necessary. It becomes necessary only when the first intention is necessary. And since it is dependent on understanding the first, when the transfer to the metaphorical is only dependent on the understanding of the first, i.e., mere understanding of the literal meaning, then the understanding of the first is only dependent on the words being correct from an Arabic language standpoint. So for transfer to the metaphorical, the speech being correct from an Arabic standpoint is sufficient, and correctness of meaning and possibility are not required. When the literal meaning is understood from the words, but its intention is not possible, whether because the slave is known lineage or older than the master, then it will be known that the intended meaning is its necessary concomitant. And the necessary concomitant of 'my son' is emancipation. So emancipation is intended. From the time he owned him, i.e., from when he became his owner, and this is the necessary concomitant of sonship. So this utterance is considered an acknowledgement of emancipation, and this slave will be freed by judicial decree without intention, because it is determined; this meaning is the only one present at that time, and there is no possibility of any other meaning, so the intention of the speaker is not considered. And he does not become free by saying 'O my son.' If instead of 'this is my son' he says to his slave 'O my son' in the form of address, then in that case the slave is not freed, because address is only for the purpose of summoning the addressee in the form of a name, and the meaning of the words is not intended. Therefore, metaphor does not operate for the sake of correcting the meaning, so there is no need for correcting the meaning. For metaphor occurs first in meaning, then in words through the mediation of meaning. When the word 'lion' is used metaphorically for a brave man, first the meaning of the word 'lion', i.e., a specific beast, is metaphorically used for the brave man, then through this metaphor, the word 'lion' is used metaphorically for the brave man. Since metaphor occurs first in meaning, second in words, metaphor does not operate in proper nouns because in a proper noun the meaning is not intended. However, those words that conventionally indicate a specific meaning, like the word 'generous' which indicates generosity, can be used metaphorically. Verified by his saying 'O free man.' Then an objection is raised: when 'O my son' in the form of address does not prove emancipation, then 'O free man' should also not prove it. He answered that by 'O free man' he becomes free, because this word is coined for the meaning of freedom and emancipation, and it has become like a proper noun for removing slavery. Therefore, no metaphor or figurative speech is involved here. Question before: It is mentioned in the science of eloquence that metaphor does not operate in the predicate of a subject. So this is not metaphor, but rather a simile without a tool of simile, called 'eloquent simile', because one: it is a claim of an impossible thing, and second: reports can be verified or falsified, and when a report is of such impossible things, for its correctness one must assume an implicit tool of simile so that the report emerges from impossibility to correctness. Therefore, 'Zayd is a lion' must be considered as 'Zayd is like a lion.' So this becomes a simile, not a metaphor. Therefore, 'this is my son' because it is similar speech, and the word 'my son' is a predicate of the subject, so it cannot be metaphor and objection. Rather, it becomes a simile with an implicit 'like': 'this is like my son', and in simile emancipation does not occur. And metaphor occurs where the thing being compared is omitted and the thing compared to is mentioned, like 'I saw a lion shooting an arrow' — here the object of comparison, i.e., a brave man, is omitted, and the thing compared to (the word lion) is mentioned, so by the context of archery, this is a metaphor for the brave man, because a real lion does not shoot arrows, but a brave man does. And even if this is impossible, it is still a metaphor. By the context, the prepositional phrase is related to the metaphor, but it is not intended. Although its literal meaning is impossible, it is not intended, because the main intended meaning at this point is the action, which is not impossible. So based on this
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul sharh discusses a key principle: when a literal meaning (haqiqah) is impossible, the utterance transfers to its metaphorical meaning (majaz) if such a transfer is possible. The discussion begins with two examples from oaths: (1) swearing to touch the sky (impossible, so no expiation), and (2) swearing to drink water from a jug that has no water (impossible at the moment, but the act of drinking is generally possible, so the oath is contracted and expiation is required if broken). The text clarifies that the transfer from literal to metaphorical depends only on the understanding (fahm) of the literal meaning, not on the intention (irada) of the speaker, because one cannot intend both literal and metaphorical meanings simultaneously. Then it applies this to the case of a master saying to his slave 'this is my son'—since literal sonship is impossible, the utterance is taken as an acknowledgement of emancipation (i'taq), because emancipation is the necessary concomitant of sonship. However, if the master says 'O my son' (in vocative form), the slave is not freed because the vocative is merely for calling, not for asserting a meaning. The text then delves into the nature of metaphor (isti'ara), explaining that metaphor operates first in meaning then in words, and thus does not apply to proper nouns (a'lam) where meaning is not intended. It also distinguishes between metaphor and eloquent simile (tashbih baligh), noting that in a predicate like 'this is my son,' the intended meaning is a simile rather than a metaphor, and simile does not produce emancipation. Finally, true metaphor requires the thing compared (musabbah) to be omitted, as in 'I saw a lion shooting an arrow,' where 'lion' metaphorically refers to a brave man. The overall principle is that when literal meaning is contextually impossible but a metaphorical meaning is accessible and contextually supported, the utterance is understood metaphorically.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal or real meaning for which a word was originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or transferred meaning that is not the original coinage but is contextually understood.
استعارة (isti'ara): A metaphor where a word is used for something other than its original meaning due to a resemblance, with the original meaning omitted.
تشبيه بليغ (tashbih baligh): An eloquent simile where the tool of comparison is omitted, implying that the subject is like the predicate.
يمين (yamin): An oath or vow; in this context, an oath to perform an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul rule discussed in this lesson, when does a statement like 'this is my son' (said by a master to his slave) result in emancipation?
A) Always, regardless of context.
B) Only if the master explicitly intends to free the slave.
C) When the literal meaning is impossible and the metaphorical meaning (emancipation) is contextually understood.
D) Only if the slave is a minor.

Key Takeaway
When literal meaning is impossible, Hanafi usul transfers to metaphorical meaning based on contextual understanding, not speaker intention.

Preference of Haqiqah over Majaz When Haqiqah is Possible
Explaining the rule for preferring literal meaning when used with known metaphorical meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for preferring haqiqah over majaz when haqiqah is used, explain the ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the rule to oath examples.

Core Idea
The original (haqiqah) is not abandoned without necessity; but if majaz is well-known, the Sahibayn defer to custom.

This passage presents a fundamental usul al-fiqh rule: when the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word is used and a metaphorical meaning (majaz) is well-known, which is preferred? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the literal meaning is more appropriate because the original is not abandoned without necessity. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) prefer the well-known metaphorical meaning, relying on customary usage. This difference has practical consequences, as illustrated by the example of an oath on wheat. Understanding this rule helps you analyze how Hanafi jurists resolve conflicts between literal and customary meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its basis
Extract the core principle: when haqiqah is used and majaz is known, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that haqiqah is awla (more appropriate) because the original (asl) is not abandoned without necessity.
This is a qiyas: haqiqah is the asl, majaz is the far'.
2
Understand the qiyas reasoning
The literal meaning is the original coinage; the metaphorical is a transfer. The original takes precedence over the subsidiary unless there is a clear preventer (qarinah) making the literal impossible.
The asl-far' analogy is key to the Hanafi position.
3
Recognize the ikhtilaf
The Sahibayn disagree: when the metaphorical meaning is well-known (muta'arif), custom ('urf) determines interpretation. They prioritize the common understanding over the literal original.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Apply to the wheat oath example
Consider the oath 'لا يأكل من هذه الحنطة' (he will not eat from this wheat). Literally, eating means chewing the grains. Metaphorically, it includes consuming bread made from the wheat. Apply each position: Imam says only chewing breaks the oath; Sahibayn say any consumption breaks it.
This thamarah al-khilaf shows the practical difference.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The same factual scenario yields different rulings: under Imam, eating bread does not break the oath; under Sahibayn, it does. This impacts fatwa and legal outcomes.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate and both views are transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کہیں عرف عام جیسے لفظ را بہ یا عرف خاص یعنی اصطلاح مخصوص جیسے صلوۃ وغیرہ میں اور توکیل بالخصومة میں قرینہ شرعیہ موجود ہے کہ مطلق خصومتہ ممنوع ہے صرف خصومة بالحق جائز ہے ۔ فان قيل لا نسلمان المعنى الحقيقي ممتنع في قوله ان لا يا كل من هذه النخلة یعنی اس کلام میں ہم قرینہ حسی مانع از ارادة الحقیقہ ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کیونکہ مخلوف علیہ عدم اکل النخلہ ہے اور عدم اکل تو حسا ممتنع نہیں بلکہ عدم اکل تو صحیح ہے البتہ اس کا اکل ممنوع ہے نہ کہ عدم اكل قلنا الیمین یعنی یمین جس وقت نفی پر داخل ہو تو وہ منع کرتی ہے عما لم یکن منوعا فموجب اليمين تو موجب یمین یہ ہوتا ہے کہ وہ شئے صرف یمین کی وجہ سے ممنون ہے اور جوشی ماکول حب و عادہ نہیں ہے وہ ممنوع بالیمین نہیں ہوتی۔ لہذا اب منع عن الاکل ہوگی لا عن عدم الاكل - فاما اذا كانت الحقيقة مستعملة يعنى جب حقیقہ مستعمل ہو اور اس کا مجاز متعارف ہو تو ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک معنی حقیقی اولی ہو گا ۔ لان الاصل لا يترك لو یعنی اصل بغیر ضرورۃ کے متروک نہیں ہو سکتا تو جب حقیقہ مستعمل ہے تو اس اصل کو ترک کر کے رجوع الی المجانہ جو کہ فرع ہے نہیں ہو سکتا ۔ وعندهما اور صاحبین کے نزدیک معنی مجازی جو متعارف ہے وہی اولی ہے۔ نظیر و لا يا كل من هذه المحنطة الا چونکہ گندم کے دانے چبا کر کھانا مستعمل ہے جو کہ حقیقت ہے اور مجاز متعارف اکل باطنہا ہے یعنی جو اس کے مافیہا سے حاصل ہو تو امام کے نزدیک قضم یعنی دانے چبا کر کھانے سے حانث ہو گا اور اس کی روٹی کھانے سے جانت نہیں ہوگا صاحبین کے نزدیک اکل ما فیہا سے حانث ہو جائے گا جس صورت میں ہو۔
Sometimes there is a customary general usage like the word 'rabb' or a specific custom like terminology such as 'salat' etc., and in the case of appointing an attorney for litigation there is a shari'ah indicator that absolute litigation is prohibited, only litigation with right is permissible. If it is said: We do not concede that the literal meaning is impossible in his statement 'he will not eat from this palm tree'—i.e., in this speech we do not accept a sensory preventing factor preventing the literal meaning, because the thing sworn about is non-eating of the palm tree, and non-eating is not sensorily impossible; rather non-eating is correct, but its eating is prohibited, not non-eating. We reply: The oath, when it enters upon negation, prohibits what was not prohibited. So the effect of the oath is that the thing is prohibited only because of the oath, and what is not customarily eaten is not prohibited by the oath. Hence now it will be prohibition of eating, not prohibition of non-eating. As for when the literal meaning is used, i.e., when the literal meaning is used and its metaphorical meaning is well-known, then according to Abu Hanifa the literal meaning is more appropriate, because the original is not abandoned without necessity. So when the literal meaning is used, this original cannot be abandoned to refer to the metaphorical which is subsidiary. And according to them (the two companions), the well-known metaphorical meaning is more appropriate. Example: 'and he will not eat from this wheat'—since chewing wheat grains is common, which is literal, and the well-known metaphorical meaning is eating its inner, i.e., what is obtained from its inside, so according to Imam, breaking (chewing) the grains will cause him to break his oath, but eating bread from it will not cause him to break his oath. According to the two companions, eating what is inside it will cause him to break his oath in whichever form it occurs.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is an usul rule about preferring literal (haqiqah) over metaphorical (majaz) meaning when the literal is used. The author begins with an example of an oath: someone swears 'he will not eat from this palm tree'. The literal meaning is impossible because one cannot eat the entire tree; the oath is interpreted as prohibiting eating its fruit (metaphorical). An objection is raised that non-eating is not impossible, but the response clarifies that the oath prohibits what was previously not prohibited, and eating is what is prohibited, not non-eating. Then the rule is stated: when the literal meaning is actually used and the metaphorical is well-known (muta'arif), Imam Abu Hanifa says the literal is more appropriate because the original (haqiqah) is not abandoned without necessity. The two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the well-known metaphorical meaning is more appropriate. The example of wheat: 'he will not eat from this wheat'. Literally eating the grains by chewing is haqiqah; metaphorically, eating bread from the flour is majaz. Imam holds that only chewing the grains breaks the oath, while the Sahibayn hold that any consumption of the wheat (like bread) breaks the oath. This is a clear madhhab difference. The authority weight: this is a standard usul rule from the classical Hanafi works; the positions of Imam and Sahibayn are from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word as originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning transferred from the literal by common usage or custom.
عرف (urf): Customary usage, whether general or specific (technical).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word is used and its metaphorical meaning (majaz) is well-known, which is preferred?
A) The literal meaning because the original is not abandoned without necessity.
B) The metaphorical meaning because it is more common.
C) Both are equally valid.
D) Neither; the statement becomes meaningless.

Key Takeaway
When haqiqah is used, it is preferred over majaz according to Imam Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn prefer well-known majaz based on custom.

When Both Haqiqah and Majaz Are Impossible: The Example of 'My Daughter'
Analyzing a case where neither literal nor metaphorical meaning is possible, leading to nullity of speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a statement where both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible and determine that it is legally ineffective (laghw) according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
When neither haqiqah nor majaz is possible, speech is laghw—legally meaningless.

In Hanafi usul, every speech is presumed to have a valid interpretation—either literal (haqiqah) or metaphorical (majaz). But what if both are impossible? In this lesson, we examine a classic example: a man calling his wife 'my daughter'. We will see why neither meaning can hold, leading to the ruling that the speech is laghw (void of legal effect).

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning (haqiqah)
Determine the literal meaning of 'my daughter'—it would establish nasab (lineage). Understand why it is impossible in two scenarios: when the wife is older (age impossibility) or when she is already known as another's daughter (established lineage cannot be negated).
The literal meaning fails because it conflicts with reality or established lineage.
2
Examine the metaphorical meaning (majaz)
Consider what metaphorical meaning might be intended—usually tahrim (prohibition) as a daughter is mahram. Explain why this too is impossible because it contradicts the nature of marriage; the word 'my daughter' indicates a type of prohibition that would nullify the marriage, not be a right within it.
The metaphorical meaning fails because it would be logically inconsistent with a valid marriage.
3
Conclude that speech is laghw
Since both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible, the statement has no legal effect—no nasab, no tahrim, no divorce. The speech is considered laghw (meaningless).
This is the default ruling when no valid interpretation survives.
4
Compare with the slave example
Contrast this with saying 'my slave' to one's own slave: literal ownership is impossible, but metaphorical meaning (freedom) is possible, so freedom is established. Understand why the two cases differ: in the slave case, one meaning (majaz) is possible; here both are impossible.
The slave case shows that if any valid meaning remains, speech is not laghw.
5
Apply the rule
State the usul rule: If both haqiqah and majaz are impossible, the speech is laghw. This principle is applied when interpreting ambiguous statements in fiqh, especially in marriage contracts and declarations.
This rule is specific to cases where no other contextual clue resolves the meaning.

Issue: Sometimes the literal meaning and the metaphorical meaning both become impossible, so then this speech will become meaningless. Like his statement to his wife 'mother'—for example, a person says to his wife, and this wife is older than the husband in age, or she is young in the house but known in lineage, i.e., people know and it is famous that she is the daughter of such-and-such person who is not the husband, so the husband says to such a wife—whether she is older or known in lineage—'my daughter'. Here neither the literal meaning nor the metaphorical is possible. As for the literal meaning: in the first case when the wife is older than the husband, it is completely obvious that the literal meaning (that she is his daughter) is impossible. And in the second case—i.e., when she is young and known in lineage—because the literal meaning, i.e., the establishment of lineage, either is established absolutely—i.e., in her right and in the right of the one from whom the woman's lineage is famous—meaning this husband's claim of lineage is established in her right and in the right of the person from whom the woman's lineage is famous, both being legally valid. The explanation of both being valid is that the lineage of this woman from this husband (the acknowledger) would be established, and from the one from whom the lineage is famous it would be negated. And this is impossible because the lineage of a woman known in lineage is already established from that famous person, so it cannot be negated merely by the husband's claim. Or (the literal meaning) is only in his own right—i.e., this claim is valid only in the husband's right, meaning lineage is established from him and not negated from the famous one? And this is impossible because pure Shari'ah would belie the husband. Because this lineage has already become famous from another, and is legally established from him. So the belittlement of the Shari'ah cannot be of a lower degree than if the person himself belied himself—i.e., after claiming lineage, he says 'I lied'. So when this self-belittling is valid, then the Shari'ah's belittling of this person is a fortiori valid. And lineage can admit of belittling and retraction. Unlike the case of freeing a slave—i.e., when he says similar words regarding his slave or slave-girl, he is considered a liar and does not intend to actually free, so the answer is that unlike the issue of right (haqq) which does not admit of belittling and retraction, hence freedom would be established. As for the metaphorical meaning, it is also impossible here, and it is prohibition (tahrim), i.e., this wife becomes unlawful for him. Because the prohibition that is established with this word contradicts the ownership of marriage, so it cannot be from the rights of marriage. Explanation: If prohibition is established with this word, then it is either a prohibition that necessitates the validity of the prior marriage or a prohibition that does not necessitate the validity of the prior marriage. The second branch is negated because if these words are said to a foreign woman who is known in lineage, the speech would be meaningless and no ruling would be established. When said to his own wife, it would a fortiori be meaningless. So it becomes known that if prohibition is established, it would be the prohibition that necessitates the validity of the prior marriage and is one of the rights of marriage, like divorce. And that is also impossible because this word 'my daughter' indicates a prohibition that necessitates the nullification of an established marriage, so how can prohibition be established with this word that is one of the rights of marriage? And know that the explanation of Fakhr al-Islam on this point is as follows: The literal meaning, i.e., the establishment of lineage, either is established in his right—i.e., in the right of the husband-acknowledger, meaning the woman's lineage is established from him and negated from the one from whom it is famous—this is impossible. Or the literal meaning is only in his own right—then this meaning is either established in the right of lineage...
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a specific problem in usul: when both the literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings of a statement are impossible, the speech becomes meaningless (laghw). The example is a man calling his wife 'my daughter' (binti). The literal meaning (haqiqah) is that she is his biological daughter, which is impossible either because she is older (impossible by age) or because she is already known as someone else's daughter (established lineage cannot be negated). The metaphorical meaning (majaz) is prohibition (tahrim) — i.e., she becomes unlawful to him like a daughter. But this prohibition cannot be established because it either implies the marriage was valid (like divorce) or invalid. The word 'my daughter' implies invalidity, so it cannot be a valid form of prohibition like divorce. The text discusses the impossibility in detail, including references to Fakhr al-Islam's explanation. A comparison is made with the case of saying 'my slave' to one's slave — there the literal (ownership) is impossible, but the metaphorical (freedom) is possible, so freedom is established. But here, both are impossible, so nothing results. The authority weight: this is a classical Hanafi usul discussion attributed to Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi) and is from the mainstream Hanafi usul works.

Key Terms
لغو (laghw): Speech that is meaningless or legally ineffective because no valid interpretation is possible.
تحریم (tahrim): Prohibition; here meant as making the wife unlawful like a mahram.
نسب (nasab): Lineage; the literal meaning of 'my daughter' would establish a father-daughter relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) His wife becomes unlawful to him (tahrim).
B) His wife is considered his daughter for inheritance purposes.
C) The speech is meaningless (laghw) with no legal effect.
D) It is considered a form of divorce.

Key Takeaway
When both literal and metaphorical meanings are impossible, the speech is laghw and carries no legal consequences.

The Preposition 'Fi' as a Container: Implicit vs Explicit and Legal Impact
Defines the usul rule for 'fi' as container with implicit vs explicit application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that implicit 'fi' (تقدير في) indicates full coverage of the container, while explicit 'fi' allows partial coverage, to derive rulings in oaths and divorce.

Core Idea
Implicit 'fi' demands full inclusion; explicit 'fi' permits partial occupancy.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh principle regarding the preposition 'fi' (in/at) when used as a container (zarf). The Hanafi school distinguishes between its explicit mention and its omission (taqdir). Correctly understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving rulings in legal contexts such as oaths, vows, and divorce pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic concept of 'zarf'
The preposition 'fi' makes its object a container that holds the action, either in time (e.g., Thursday) or space (e.g., jug). Understand that the container is meant to be inclusive of the action.
The Arabic term 'zarf' literally means a vessel or envelope.
2
Distinguish explicit vs implicit 'fi'
Look at the examples: 'samt hadhihi al-sana' (I fasted this year) vs 'sawmt fi hadhihi al-sana' (I fasted in this year). In the first, the preposition 'fi' is omitted (implicit); in the second, it is explicit.
When 'fi' is omitted, the time/place is in the accusative case, acting like a direct object.
3
Apply the rule: implicit requires isti'āb
Implicit 'fi' demands full coverage (isti'āb) of the container. Thus 'samt hadhihi al-sana' means fasting the entire year, not just part of it.
Explicit 'fi' does not require full coverage; only a portion need be occupied.
4
Extend to legal contexts: timing of divorce
In 'anta taliq ghadan' (you are divorced tomorrow, implicit fi), divorce takes effect at the beginning of the day, covering the whole day. In 'anta taliq fi ghadin' (explicit), the husband may intend a specific part.
The rule ensures that if full coverage is intended, the ruling is applied comprehensively.
5
Handle the exception for place
When 'fi' is used with a place (e.g., 'anta taliq fi al-dar'), divorce is immediate regardless of location, because divorce cannot be restricted by place. However, if the intent is 'upon entering the house' (by ellipsis), 'fi' becomes conditional.
This exception shows that the rule for time does not always apply to space; the nature of the act matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي للظرف كلمہ نی برائے ظرف ہے یعنی اس کا مجرور ما قبل پر مشتمل ہو گا خواہ اشتمال مکانی ہو یا زمانی جیسے الماء في الكوز الصوم في يوم الخميس. والفوق ثابت الخذ اثبات کی اور اضمار فی میں فرق ہے یعنی کلمہ فی ظاہر کر کے ذکر کیا جائے اور اسے مقدر کر دیا جائے ان دونوں میں معنی فرق ہو گا نحو صمت بذه السنة بتقدير في مقتضی کل ہے یعنی مکمل سال بھر کا صوم اس نے پورا کیا ہے کیونکہ یہاں ظرف بمنزلہ مفعوان ہے کے ہو چکی ہے جو منصوب بالفعل ہے اور یہ مقتضی استیعاب ہے بخلاف صمت فی ہذہ السنہ کے کہ مقتضی استیعاب نہیں بلکہ اس سال بھر میں ایک ساعتہ کا صوم اختیار کرے بال بیوی الصوم الى اللیل تو وہ اس کلام میں صادق ہوگا فلهذا چونکہ تقدیر فی استیعاب ظرف کو چاہتا ہے تو انت طالق غدا میں طلاق اول النہار سے واقع ہو جائیگی لیکون واقعا الا تاکہ طلاق کا وقوع جميع غر میں ثابت رہے جو مقتضائے استیفا ہے اور اگر انت طالق فی الغد کہتا ہے اور نیت آخر النہار کی کرے تو صحیح ہے۔ ولو قال انت طالق في الدار تطلق حالا یعنی فی الحال مطلقہ ہو جائیگی خواہ دار میں ہو یا نہ ہو کیونکہ مکان مخصص طلاق نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ یہ منع ہے کہ طلاق ایک مکان میں واقع ہو اور دوسرے میں واقع نہ ہو۔ جب عورت مطلقہ ہوتی ہے تو وہ ہر مکان میں مطلقہ ہوتی ہے۔ لا ان بینوی مگر یہ کہ فی الدار سے مراد في دخولك الدار سبو بحذف المضاف فيتعلق به تو اس وقت طلاق دخول دار کے ساتھ متعلق ہوگی اور فی گویا برائے شرط ہو جائیگا
And 'fi' is for the container; the word 'fi' is for container, i.e., its object is inclusive of what comes before, whether spatial inclusion or temporal, like 'water in the jug', 'fasting on Thursday'. And the difference between expressing it and omitting it is that when the word 'fi' is mentioned explicitly or omitted, there is a difference in meaning. For example, 'I fasted this year' with 'fi' implied: it requires complete coverage, i.e., he fasted the entire year, because here the container is like an object that is accusative due to the verb, and this requires full coverage, unlike 'I fasted in this year' which does not require coverage; rather, he may fast one hour in this year. The same applies to the oath 'by Allah, I will fast until night'; he is truthful in that statement. Therefore, since the implied 'fi' requires full coverage of the container, so in 'you are divorced tomorrow', divorce occurs at the beginning of the day so that divorce is established for the entire day, which is required by full coverage. And if he says 'you are divorced in tomorrow' and intends the end of the day, it is valid. And if he says 'you are divorced in the house', she is divorced immediately, whether she is in the house or not, because a place cannot specify divorce, since it is forbidden that divorce occurs in one place and not another. When a woman is divorced, she is divorced in every place. But if by 'in the house' he means 'upon entering the house', omitting the object of the verb, then divorce is tied to entering the house, and 'fi' becomes for condition.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This lesson explains the usul of the preposition 'fi' (in/at) as a container (zarf). The author states that 'fi' indicates that its object contains the preceding element, whether in place or time. An important distinction is made between explicitly stating 'fi' and omitting it. When 'fi' is omitted (e.g., 'samt hadhihi al-sana' meaning 'I fasted this year'), the intended meaning is complete coverage of the time period; the year acts as an adverb of time with full inclusion. However, if 'fi' is explicit ('sawmt fi hadhihi al-sana'), it does not imply full coverage; a person could fast for just an hour and truthfully say they fasted in that year. This linguistic rule has direct applications in Islamic legal rulings, particularly in oaths and divorce. For example, if one swears 'I will not speak to Zayd today' with an implied 'fi', it covers the entire day. Similarly, in divorce, if a husband says 'you are divorced tomorrow' (with implied 'fi'), the divorce takes effect at the beginning of the day and covers the whole day. If he says 'you are divorced in tomorrow' (explicit 'fi'), the divorce is still instant but he may intend a specific part of the day. Another example: if he says 'you are divorced in the house', the divorce occurs immediately regardless of her location because divorce is a personal status not tied to place. However, if the intention is 'upon entering the house' (by ellipsis of 'dakhul' and attaching 'fi' to that), then 'fi' becomes conditional, and the divorce is suspended upon entry. This distinction is crucial for understanding how linguistic tools affect legal outcomes.

Key Terms
الظرف (al-zarf): A linguistic container (preposition) that indicates the time or place in which an action occurs; here specifically the preposition 'fi' is analyzed.
الاستیعاب (al-isti'āb): Full coverage or encompassing of the container (time or place) such that the action occupies the entire extent.
تقدیر (taqdir): Implication or ellipsis; here refers to the omission of the preposition 'fi' while its meaning is still intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his wife 'you are divorced tomorrow' (with implicit 'fi'), when does the divorce take effect according to the Hanafi school?
A) At the beginning of the day
B) At the end of the day
C) At the exact time the words are spoken
D) At midnight

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul distinction between explicit and implicit 'fi' as a container determines whether an oath or pronouncement covers the entire time/place or only a part.

Hanafi Text Clarity Hierarchy
intermediate
6 min
The Four Categories of Clear Texts: Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, Muhkam
Defining and exemplifying the Hanafi hierarchy of textual clarity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between zahir, nass, mufassar, and muhkam in a given text and identify their levels of epistemic certainty.

Core Idea
Textual clarity in Hanafi usul ranges from probable (zahir) to definitive (muhkam), each with decreasing possibilities for interpretation and abrogation.

This passage introduces a foundational taxonomy in Hanafi usul: the hierarchy of textual clarity. Understanding where a text falls on the spectrum from zahir to muhkam determines how binding its meaning is and whether it can be specified, interpreted, or even abrogated. We will work through the four categories and apply them to a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base category: Zahir
Zahir is a word whose intended meaning is apparent. However, it can still be subject to takhsis (specification) or ta'wil (interpretation). The clarity is probable, not definitive.
In Hanafi usul, zahir is zanni al-dalalah but can be strengthened by other evidence.
2
Recognize the stronger category: Nass
Nass is when the wording and context are specifically directed to that meaning. It is clearer than zahir but still permits takhsis and ta'wil. The context makes the meaning more specific.
Nass is stronger than zahir but not immune to specification.
3
Recognize the even stronger category: Mufassar
Mufassar is so clear that ta'wil and takhsis are completely closed. No alternative interpretation is possible. However, abrogation (naskh) remains possible.
Mufassar is unambiguous in meaning but can be abrogated.
4
Identify the highest category: Muhkam
Muhkam is the level of ultimate clarity. Even abrogation is not possible. The ruling is eternal and definitive.
Muhkam is the strongest form of textual clarity.
5
Apply the taxonomy to the Quranic example (2:275)
The verse 'And Allah permitted trade and prohibited usury' is zahir regarding the permissibility of trade and prohibition of usury — the meaning is clear but open to specification. It is nass regarding the distinction between trade and usury because the context (refuting the disbelievers) makes that distinction the primary point.
One text can be both zahir and nass depending on which aspect is considered.

The third division is about the clarity and obscurity of meaning. A word, when its meaning and intended sense are apparent, is called zahir relative to that intended meaning. Then, if clarity increases so that the word is directed specifically for that meaning—its context and wording are for that meaning—then such a word is called nass. But it can still have probability of takhsis and ta'wil. Then if clarity increases so that the possibility of ta'wil and takhsis is completely closed—neither ta'wil nor takhsis is possible—but abrogation is still possible, such a word is called mufassar. Then if clarity increases so that even the possibility of abrogation is closed, it is called muhkam. These are four categories based on clarity of intended meaning: zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam. An example is the verse 'And Allah permitted trade and prohibited usury.' This verse is both zahir and nass: regarding the permissibility of trade and prohibition of usury it is zahir, because people of language understand this meaning clearly, with no obscurity. Regarding differentiating between trade and usury it is nass, because its context is to distinguish between them, as it is a response to the disbelievers who said 'Trade is just like usury'—meaning both are equal in benefit—so it was replied that they are not, rather in divine law trade is halal and usury is haram.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage introduces the third division of the Hanafi usul classification of texts based on clarity: zahir, nass, mufassar, and muhkam. Zahir is when the intended meaning is apparent but may still be subject to specification (takhsis) or interpretation (ta'wil). Nass is when the text is clearly directed to that meaning, but takhsis and ta'wil are still possible. Mufassar is so clear that ta'wil and takhsis are impossible, but abrogation is still possible. Muhkam is the highest clarity, immune even to abrogation. The example given is the Quranic verse 'And Allah permitted trade and prohibited usury' (2:275). Regarding the rulings themselves (halal/haram), it is zahir; regarding the distinction between trade and usury (which is the context of refuting the disbelievers' claim of equality), it is nass.

Key Terms
ظاہر (zahir): A word whose intended meaning is apparent, though takhsis and ta'wil are possible.
نص (nass): A word whose context and wording are specifically for its meaning, but still open to takhsis and ta'wil.
مفسر (mufassar): A word so clear that ta'wil and takhsis are impossible, but naskh is still possible.
محکم (muhkam): A word with ultimate clarity, immune even to naskh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which category of text is immune to both ta'wil and naskh?
A) Zahir
B) Nass
C) Mufassar
D) Muhkam

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hierarchy of textual clarity—zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam—determines the epistemic weight of a text, from probable to definitive.

Hanafi Text Clarity Hierarchy
intermediate
12 min
The Hanafi Hierarchy of Textual Clarity: Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, Muhkam
Explaining the Hanafi hierarchy of textual clarity with Quranic examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four Hanafi categories of word clarity (Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, Muhkam) and apply them to classify Quranic expressions.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, textual clarity rises from Zahir (apparent) to Muhkam (definitive) based on the possibility of specification, interpretation, and abrogation.

This passage introduces a core Hanafi usul classification that ranks texts by their clarity. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for knowing how much interpretive flexibility remains in a Qat'i vs. Zanni text. The author defines four levels—Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, Muhkam—each with its own rules regarding takhsis (specification), ta'wil (interpretation), and naskh (abrogation). Working through the Quranic examples will sharpen your ability to evaluate the strength of legal evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Read the definitions of Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, and Muhkam. Note how each subsequent level adds a degree of clarity and restricts the possibilities of specification and interpretation.
The key is to see the hierarchy: Zahir allows both takhsis and ta'wil; Nass allows both but is more explicit due to context; Mufassar blocks takhsis and ta'wil but allows naskh; Muhkam blocks even naskh.
2
Analyze the first Quranic example
The verse 'And Allah permitted trade and prohibited usury' (2:275) is Zahir regarding the permissibility of trade and prohibition of usury. It is Nass regarding the distinction between trade and usury because the context refutes the disbelievers' claim that they are alike.
Understand how context (siyaq) elevates a text from Zahir to Nass for a specific meaning.
3
Analyze the second Quranic example
The verse 'marry what seems good to you of women, two, three, and four' (4:3) is Zahir for the permissibility of marriage (since the command is for permission, not obligation) and Nass for the number of wives allowed. The author provides two proofs that the number is the main intended meaning.
Focus on the condition: when the original act is non-obligatory, the condition becomes the main focus. If the act were obligatory, the condition would not be the primary intent.
4
Apply the condition about obligatory acts
The author introduces a key nuance: if the original act is obligatory, the condition in the command is not the main intent. Example: 'zakat al-fitr from every free and slave from the Muslims'—since zakat al-fitr is obligatory, the condition 'from the Muslims' does not restrict the obligation to Muslim slaves only; Hanafis rule that the master must pay for non-Muslim slaves as well.
This is a practical application of the same principle.
5
Recognize the Hanafi uniqueness
The categories Mufassar and Muhkam are distinctive to the Hanafi school. Other schools use different terms or combine these levels. This hierarchy directly impacts the strength of a textual proof in istinbat.
This classification is not merely theoretical; it determines whether a ruling can be specified, interpreted, or abrogated by later evidence.

The third division concerning clarity and obscurity of meaning: If the intended meaning of a word is apparent, then that word is called Zahir in relation to that meaning. Then if clarity increases such that the word is specifically directed to that meaning, i.e., its context is for that meaning, then such a word is called Nass, though it may still be open to specification (takhsis) and interpretation (ta'wil). Then if clarity increases so that the door of interpretation and specification is completely closed, i.e., there is no room for interpretation or specification, but the possibility of abrogation (naskh) remains, such a word is called Mufassar. Then if clarity and strength increase so that even the possibility of abrogation is closed, its name is Muhkam. These are the four divisions according to the clarity of the intended meaning: Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, Muhkam. Example: His statement, "And Allah permitted trade and prohibited usury" (Quran 2:275). This verse is an example of both Zahir and Nass. In terms of the permissibility of trade and prohibition of usury, it is Zahir, because the people of language understand this meaning clearly, there is no obscurity. And in terms of distinguishing between trade and usury, it is Nass, because its context is to differentiate between trade and usury, as it is a response to the statement of the disbelievers who said, "Indeed trade is like usury" (Quran 2:275). So it was refuted that it is not so, but rather in divine law, trade is halal and usury is haram. As for the details of the difference and its reasons and causes, one should refer to tafsir. And His statement, "two, three, and four" (Quran 4:3) is Zahir regarding permissibility and Nass regarding number. This is a second example of Zahir and Nass. The first verse is Zahir and Nass in terms of two meanings, and the second is in terms of two words: one word is Zahir and the other is Nass. The words "marry what seems good to you" are Zahir concerning the permissibility of marriage, because this command is not for obligation such that it would be considered Zahir in affirming obligation, but rather for permission, so it is Zahir in affirming permission and legality. And "two, three, four" — their context is for affirming number, and the intended meaning is that, so they are Nass concerning the affirmation of number. This verse is directed for affirming number, and the intended meaning of the verse is that. Two proofs were presented: first, because permissibility is known from other than this verse, such as the statement of Allah, "Lawful for you are those beyond them" (Quran 4:24), meaning besides the prohibited women, the rest are halal for you to marry. So the intended meaning of "marry what seems good to you" is not this permissibility, but rather its intended meaning is to express number, so that this verse conveys a new benefit, because giving benefit is better than repeating. And second, because when a command comes concerning something qualified by a condition, the intended meaning is that condition, and its affirmation is desired from the command, provided that the original thing is not obligatory. Example: "sell equal for equal" — since sale is not obligatory but permissible and lawful, here the intended meaning is this condition of equality, and its affirmation is desired from the command: that in exchange of usurious wealth, equality of countervalues when of the same type is necessary and obligatory. Similarly, in our case, marriage itself is not obligatory but permissible and lawful, so the intended meaning of the verse is exactly "two, three, four", and the desired from the command is the affirmation of number. Therefore, these words are at the level of Nass regarding the affirmation of number. This is based on what the Arabic grammarians have established: when speech includes a condition additional to mere affirmation or negation, that condition is the focus of benefit and the subject of affirmation or negation. We have added the condition that the original thing is not obligatory, because if the thing itself is obligatory, then the intended meaning of the command is its obligatoriness; if it includes a condition, that condition is not the intended meaning. For example, in the hadith about zakat al-fitr, "from every free and slave" — in some narrations the condition "from the Muslims" is mentioned. Since sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory, its obligatoriness is the intended meaning of the hadith; the condition "from the Muslims" is not the intended meaning. Therefore, according to the Hanafis, the master must pay sadaqat al-fitr for his slave, whether the slave is Muslim or non-Muslim. And the example of Mufassar: "Prostrate to Adam" etc. ... The first two examples.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the Hanafi division of words based on the clarity of their intended meaning. The author defines four categories: Zahir (apparent), Nass (explicit), Mufassar (detailed), and Muhkam (definitive). Zahir is a word whose meaning is immediately understood by people of language without any hiddenness. If the word is specifically directed to that meaning by its context (siyaq), it becomes Nass, though still open to specification (takhsis) or interpretation (ta'wil). When clarity increases such that no specification or interpretation is possible but abrogation (naskh) remains, it is Mufassar. When even abrogation is impossible, it is Muhkam. The author provides Quranic examples: the verse about trade and usury (2:275) is Zahir for the permissibility of trade and prohibition of usury, and Nass for distinguishing between them, as the context refutes the disbelievers' claim. Another example is the verse on marriage (4:3): the command to marry is Zahir for permissibility (since it's not obligatory), while the numbers (two, three, four) are Nass for the number of wives allowed, as the context emphasizes the limit. The author supports this with two arguments: first, permissibility of marriage is already known elsewhere, so the new benefit is the number; second, when a command qualifies a non-obligatory act, the condition becomes the focus. The discussion also clarifies that when the original act is obligatory, the condition is not the main intent, as in zakat al-fitr where the obligation applies to all slaves regardless of faith.

Key Terms
ظاهر (Zahir): A word whose intended meaning is apparent, though it may still be open to specification or interpretation.
نص (Nass): A word whose context (siyaq) is specifically directed to its meaning, though still open to specification or interpretation.
مفسر (Mufassar): A word so clear that it cannot be specified or interpreted, but may be abrogated.
محكم (Muhkam): A word whose clarity is so strong that even abrogation is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, which category of word clarity still allows the possibility of abrogation (naskh) but not specification (takhsis)?
A) Zahir
B) Nass
C) Mufassar
D) Muhkam

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hierarchy of textual clarity—Zahir, Nass, Mufassar, Muhkam—provides a nuanced way to assess the strength of textual evidence, with each level affecting the possibility of specification, interpretation, and abrogation.

The Marriage Verse: Zahir and Nass in One Text
Explaining how one verse can be both zahir and nass for different meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a single Quranic verse can function as both zahir and nass for different legal aspects, and apply the Hanafi principle that a restriction is the main objective when the primary act is not obligatory.

Core Idea
A single text can be zahir for one ruling and nass for another — the key is identifying the restriction that carries the intended new benefit.

This passage demonstrates a core Hanafi usul al-fiqh skill: analysing a single verse for multiple levels of textual clarity. The commentators use the marriage verse (an-Nisa: 3) to show how one part is zahir (apparent, open to interpretation) regarding permissibility, while another part is nass (explicit, not open to interpretation) regarding the number of wives. The reasoning involves understanding the purpose of the verse and the role of restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse and its components
Locate the verse: 'فانكحوا ما طاب لكم مثنى وثلاث ورباع'. Break it into the command part 'فانكحوا ما طاب لكم' and the numerical restriction 'مثنى وثلاث ورباع'.
2
Classify each component for textual category
According to the Hanafi analysis, the command part is zahir for permissibility (not obligation) because the act of marriage is already known to be permissible from other evidence. The numerical restriction is nass for the number because it provides a new specific limit.
The classification depends on external evidence and the principle of giving new benefit.
3
Apply the principle: restriction is the main objective
Apply the Hanafi rule: when a command comes with a restriction (qayd), and the primary commanded act is not obligatory (like marriage is mubah), then the restriction is the main objective intended by the command. This is because the act itself is already known, so the restriction brings new benefit.
The exception: if the primary act is obligatory, then the restriction does not become the main objective (e.g., sadaqat al-fitr hadith).
4
Confirm by checking if the primary act's ruling is already established elsewhere
The permissibility of marriage is already established by other verses (e.g., 'احل لكم ما وراء ذلكم'). Thus, the purpose of this verse is not to command marriage but to specify the number limit.
This step uses the principle 'الإفادة خير من الإعادة' (giving benefit is better than repetition).
5
Derive the final ruling
The verse yields two rulings: (1) marriage is permissible (zahir, not obligatory); (2) the maximum number of wives is four (nass, explicit and binding).

The explanation and its causes and reasons: refer to commentaries. And His saying 'two, three, and four' is zahir regarding permissibility and nass regarding number. This is the second example of zahir and nass. The first verse in terms of specific meanings was zahir and nass, and here in terms of words: one word is zahir and another is nass. 'Marry what is good for you' words are zahir regarding permissibility of marriage because this command is not for obligation that it would be considered zahir in establishing obligation, but rather for permission. So it is zahir in establishing permission and permissibility. Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Quranic verse 'فانكحوا ما طاب لكم مثنى وثلاث ورباع' (Marry what is good for you: two, three, and four). The commentator explains that this single verse combines two levels of textual clarity: it is nass (explicit) regarding the number of wives (establishing that one may marry up to four) and it is zahir (apparent) regarding the permissibility of marriage itself. The reasoning is that the permissibility of marriage is already established elsewhere (e.g., 'احل لكم ما وراء ذلكم'), so the purpose of this verse is to specify the number. The commentator provides two proofs: first, that a new benefit (number) is intended; second, that when a command comes for something with a restriction, the restriction is the main objective if the primary act is not obligatory. He draws an analogy to the command 'بيعوا سواء بسواء' (sell equally for equal), where the equality restriction is the objective. He then clarifies an exception: if the primary act itself is obligatory, like sadaqat al-fitr, then the restriction (e.g., 'من المسلمين') does not become the main objective; hence, the ruling that the master must pay fitra for his slave regardless of the slave's religion. This passage illustrates the Hanafi usul principle that a single text can have different levels of clarity for different aspects, and it demonstrates how to determine which part is the intended objective.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-zahir): اللفظ الذي يحتمل التأويل ولكنه راجح في معناه الظاهر
النص (al-nass): اللفظ الذي لا يحتمل التأويل ولا التخصيص في معناه

A single text can have multiple levels of textual clarity: the marriage verse is nass for the number of wives and zahir for the permissibility of marriage, based on the principle that restrictions carry the intended legal benefit.

The Four Levels of Textual Obscurity in Hanafi Usul
Definitions of the four obscure text categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four levels of textual obscurity (khafi, mushkil, mujmal, mutashabih) and state their definitions and causes according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Textual clarity has four opposites: khafi (external cause), mushkil (inherent but resolvable), mujmal (needs transmission), mutashabih (unknowable).

In Hanafi usul, texts are classified by clarity. After studying the four clear levels (zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam), we now examine their opposites. Understanding these categories is essential for knowing when a text's meaning is hidden, why, and how to resolve it.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four clear levels
Start by listing the four clear text categories: zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam. Each has a specific level of clarity.
These are the 'opposites' of the obscure levels we are about to learn.
2
Identify the cause of obscurity
For each obscure level, the text explains the cause: khafi from an external factor, mushkil from inherent obscurity in the meaning, mujmal from needing transmitted explanation, mutashabih from absolute unknowability.
Pay attention to the key distinction between khafi (external) and mushkil (inherent).
3
Match the opposite pairs
Memorize the pairings: opposite of zahir is khafi, of nass is mushkil, of mufassar is mujmal, of muhkam is mutashabih.
This parallel structure helps in grasping the hierarchy.
4
Define each term precisely
Write the definition of each obscure level as given in the text, noting the method of resolution: khafi resolved by looking for excess/deficiency in meaning, mushkil by rational reflection, mujmal by transmitted report, mutashabih not resolvable.
The example of khafi with the theft verse will be detailed in the next lesson.

نباش کفن دزد کو کہتے ہیں اور طرار جیب تراش کو کہتے ہیں اگر چہ دونوں کا فعل از قسم سرقتہ تو ہے لیکن ان کا نام الگ الگ ہو چکا ہے انھیں سارق کے نام سے موسوم نہیں کیا جاتا اب لفظ سارق ان کے حق میں خفی ہو چکا ہے کہ سارق سے مراد نباش و طرار ہو سکتے ہیں یا نہیں اور یہ خضار بسبب اسی عارض کے ہے کہ ان کا اسم الگ الگ ہو چکا ہے اور یہ اپنے اسی اسم کے ساتھ مختص ہیں ۔ اب اسی خفاء کی بنا پر حکم قطع میں خفا ہوا کہ ان پر قطع ید ہو یا نہ ہو تو اس کے متعلق کہا کہ فينظران كان الخفاء لمزية الجاب دیکھا جائے گا کہ جو لفظ ان کے حق میں خفی ہو رہا ہے اگر اس کی وجہ یہ ہو کہ اس لفظ کے معنی سے اس میں کچھ مزیہ اور زیادہ موجود ہے تو پثبت له الحکم تو حکم اس زیادہ والے کے لئے ثابت ہو جائیگا۔ و لنقصان اگر اس میں وہ معنی ناقص ہے تو لو پھر حکم اس کے لئے ثابت نہیں ہو گا تو ہم نے دیکھا کہ سرقہ کا معنی ہے اخذ المال المملوک من المحرز خفیہ بغفلة صاحبہ اور طرار تو اس لحاظ سے کامل سارق ہے لانہ یا خذا المال المملوك مع حضور الممالک ویقظتہ تو جب اس کے اندر معنی سرقہ علی طریق الکمال موجود ہے تو اس پر حکم سرقتہ بطریق اولی عائد ہو گا لہذا اس پر قطع ید ہے بخلاف نباش کے کہ اس کے اندر معنی سرقتة على سبيل النقص ہے کیونکہ نباش تو موتی کے کفن اتارلیتا ہے ولا تكون المحافظة والمحرز بالموتى لہذا اس پر حد سرقہ قائم نہیں ہوگی کیونکہ حدود مندر کی بالشبہات ہو جاتی ہیں اور اس کے سارق ہونے میں شبھہ واقع ہو چکا ہے کہ اس کا فعل معنی سرقہ سے ناقص اور کمزور ہے۔ والمشكل اما لغموض في المعنی مشکل وہ تھا جس میں خفا نفس لفظ کے اندر تھا لیکن اس کا ادراک عقل سے ہو سکتا تھا اس کے اشکال کی وجہ پیش کرتے ہوئے کہا اما لغموض في المعنی کہ اس معنی میں غموض اور پوشیدگی ہوگی اور یہی موجب اشکال ہوگی نحو وان كنتم جنبا فاطعہ وا نظر کا معنی ہے
And when it is hidden (khafi) – from here [the text] presents the opposites of zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam; they are also four. The opposite of zahir is khafi, of nass is mushkil, of mufassar is mujmal, and of muhkam is mutashabih. So khafi is due to an external cause: i.e., the intended meaning of the word becomes hidden due to some external cause; meaning that in terms of the word itself there is no hiddenness, but it occurs due to an external cause. It is called khafi. As for khafi in itself, i.e., hiddenness of meaning is due to the word itself and its essence, so this is inherent hiddenness. Then it is considered: if its meaning can be known and perceived by reason, then it is mushkil – its meaning can be known by reflection and contemplation with reason. If it cannot be perceived by reason but by transmitted report (naql) – then it is mujmal: the meaning of mujmal is not known by reason, but its meaning is known by transmitted report from the Lawgiver. If it cannot be perceived at all, neither by reason nor by transmission, then it is mutashabih. So khafi: like the verse of theft (sariqa) – this is khafi regarding the grave-robber (nabbash) and the pickpocket (tarrar). Nabbash is one who steals shrouds, and tarrar is one who cuts pockets. Although the act of both is from the category of theft, yet they have separate names; they are not called by the name 'sāriq' (thief). Now the word 'sāriq' has become khafi with respect to them: whether sāriq can mean nabbash and tarrar or not. And this hiddenness is due to the external cause that they have separate names and are specified with their own name. Now due to this hiddenness, hiddenness occurred in the ruling of amputation: whether amputation of the hand applies to them or not. Regarding this it is said: 'So it is considered: if the hiddenness is due to an excess of meaning' – it will be seen that the word that is khafi with respect to them, if the cause is that there is some excess and more in its meaning, then the ruling is established for that excess. And if [the meaning] is deficient, then the ruling is not established for it. So we see that the meaning of theft is 'taking property of another covertly from its secure place without the owner's knowledge'. And the pickpocket (tarrar) in this respect is a complete thief because he takes the property in the presence of the owner and while the owner is awake. So when the meaning of theft exists in him completely, then the ruling of theft applies a fortiori; therefore, amputation of the hand is on him. Unlike the grave-robber (nabbash), because the meaning of theft in him is deficient: the nabbash takes the shroud from the dead, and there is no guarding and secure place with the dead. Therefore, the hadd penalty of theft is not established on him because hadd penalties are averted by doubts, and doubt has occurred regarding his being a thief, since his act is deficient and weak in the meaning of theft. As for mushkil: it is due to obscurity in the meaning. Mushkil is that which has hiddenness in the word itself, but its perception can be attained by reason. Explaining the cause of its difficulty, he said: 'either due to obscurity in the meaning' – i.e., there is hiddenness and concealment in the meaning, and that is the cause of difficulty, like the verse: 'And if you are in a state of janabah, then purify yourselves [by washing]' and its meaning...
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the four levels of textual clarity and their opposites, as part of the Hanafi usul hierarchy. The text explains that each of the four clear levels (zahir, nass, mufassar, muhkam) has a corresponding hidden level: khafi (hidden due to an external cause), mushkil (difficult due to inherent obscurity but resolvable by reason), mujmal (ambiguous, requiring transmitted explanation), and mutashabih (absolutely obscure, known only to Allah). The text then delves into khafi with a detailed example from the law of theft (sariqa). The word 'sāriq' is clear in itself, but becomes khafi when applied to specialized thieves like nabbash (grave-robber) and tarrar (pickpocket) because common usage gives them distinct names. The ruling of amputation for theft (cutting of hand) is then examined: the pickpocket, who steals openly from a person's pocket while the owner is awake, fully meets the definition of theft (taking property covertly from its secure place without owner's knowledge), so the hadd applies. The grave-robber, however, does not steal from a 'secure place' (hirz) because the dead body's shroud is not considered guarded; thus, the penalty is averted due to doubt (shubhah). The passage also introduces mushkil with the example of the Quranic verse about janabah (state of major impurity) and purification, noting that its apparent meaning might be obscure but can be clarified through rational reflection. The overall lesson establishes how Hanafi usul categorizes texts and derives rulings when clarity is compromised.

Key Terms
الخفي (al-khafi): A text whose meaning is hidden due to an external cause (e.g., common usage restricts a general term)
المشكل (al-mushkil): A text whose meaning is inherently obscure but can be understood through rational reflection
المجمل (al-mujmal): A text whose meaning is not known by reason but requires transmitted explanation from the Lawgiver
المتشابه (al-mutashabih): A text whose meaning is completely unknown, neither by reason nor transmission

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a khafi text from a mushkil text according to the passage?
A) Khafi is resolved by reason; mushkil requires transmitted explanation
B) Khafi is caused by an external factor; mushkil is due to inherent obscurity in the word
C) Khafi applies only to the verse of theft; mushkil applies to all other verses
D) Khafi is the same as mujmal

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the classification of a text's clarity depends on the cause of its hiddenness: external (khafi), inherent but resolvable (mushkil), needing transmission (mujmal), or unknowable (mutashabih).

Hanafi Text Clarity Hierarchy

Resolving Khafi: The Theft Verse and Ruling on Nabbash and Tarrar
Detailed application of khafi resolution to the theft verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of excess/deficiency (maziyyah/nuqsan) to resolve a khafi text and derive the correct ruling, using the theft example as a model.

Core Idea
When a word is khafi due to custom, the ruling follows the perfect meaning and is averted by doubt from the deficient.

The passage uses the word 'sāriq' (thief) to demonstrate how to handle a khafi text. The word is clear in its general meaning, but when applied to specialized thieves like the grave-robber (nabbash) and pickpocket (tarrar), its application becomes hidden. The Hanafi method looks at whether the thief's act perfectly matches the definition of theft; if it does, the hadd applies; if deficient, the hadd is averted due to doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the khafi text and the external cause
The word 'sāriq' is clear in general, but becomes khafi for nabbash and tarrar because they have distinct names, creating an external cause of hiddenness.
The external cause is the separate naming in custom.
2
Analyze the meaning of the word (sariq) in its complete sense
Define theft (sariqah) as 'taking property of another covertly from its secure place (hirz) without the owner's knowledge'. Then evaluate each criminal against this definition.
The definition is crucial to assess completeness.
3
Apply the principle of excess/deficiency
If the criminal's act perfectly embodies the meaning (as in tarrar, who steals from a pocket while owner is awake), the ruling (hadd) applies a fortiori. If the meaning is deficient (as in nabbash, who takes from a dead body lacking hirz), the ruling is averted due to doubt.
This principle is stated in the text: 'fayunzaru in kāna al-khafā' li maziyyah' (so it is considered: if the hiddenness is due to excess, the ruling is established; if deficiency, it is not).
4
Derive the ruling for each case
State: For tarrar, amputation applies because he is a complete thief. For nabbash, amputation does not apply because the meaning of theft is deficient, creating doubt (shubhah) that averts the hadd.
The text says 'al-hudūd tuḍra' bi al-shubuhāt' – hadd penalties are averted by doubts.

Hanafi Text Clarity Hierarchy
intermediate
